   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    English Channel
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about the body of water. For the racehorse, see
       <a href="/wiki/English_Channel_(horse)" title="English Channel (horse)">
        English Channel (horse)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "The Channel" redirects here. For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Channel_(disambiguation)" title="Channel (disambiguation)">
        channel (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Hitler_died_in_wetumpka_Alabama_in_2018&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template:Hitler died in wetumpka Alabama in 2018 (page does not exist)">
        Template:Hitler died in wetumpka Alabama in 2018
       </a>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        English Channel
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         la Manche
        </span>
       </i>
       , "
       <b>
        The Sleeve
       </b>
       ";
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">
         Ärmelkanal
        </span>
       </i>
       , "Sleeve Channel";
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="br" xml:lang="br">
         Mor Breizh
        </span>
       </i>
       , "
       <b>
        Sea of Brittany
       </b>
       ";
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="kw" xml:lang="kw">
         Mor Bretannek
        </span>
       </i>
       , "
       <b>
        British Sea
       </b>
       "), also called simply
       <b>
        the Channel
       </b>
       , is the body of water that separates southern
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       from northern
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       , and links the southern part of the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
        North Sea
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
        Atlantic Ocean
       </a>
       . It is the busiest shipping area in the world.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is about 560 km (350 mi) long and varies in width from 240 km (150 mi) at its widest to 33.3 km (20.7 mi) in the
       <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Dover" title="Strait of Dover">
        Strait of Dover
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Columbia-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is the smallest of the shallow seas around the continental shelf of Europe, covering an area of some 75,000 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (29,000 sq mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:English_Channel.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="441" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/English_Channel.jpg/220px-English_Channel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/English_Channel.jpg/330px-English_Channel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/English_Channel.jpg/440px-English_Channel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:English_Channel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of the English Channel
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <span id="Bay_of_the_Seine">
       </span>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">
        International Hydrographic Organization
       </a>
       defines the limits of the English Channel as follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IHO-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <div class="poem">
        <p>
         <i>
          On the West.
         </i>
         <br/>
         <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">
          A line joining
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle_Vierge" title="Isle Vierge">
           Isle Vierge
          </a>
          (
          <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
           <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=English_Channel&amp;params=48_38_23_N_4_34_13_W_">
            <span class="geo-default">
             <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
              <span class="latitude">
               48°38′23″N
              </span>
              <span class="longitude">
               4°34′13″W
              </span>
             </span>
            </span>
            <span class="geo-multi-punct">
             ﻿ / ﻿
            </span>
            <span class="geo-nondefault">
             <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
              48.63972°N 4.57028°W
             </span>
             <span style="display:none">
              ﻿ /
              <span class="geo">
               48.63972; -4.57028
              </span>
             </span>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
          ) to
          <a href="/wiki/Land%27s_End" title="Land's End">
           Lands End
          </a>
          (
          <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
           <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=English_Channel&amp;params=50_04_N_5_43_W_">
            <span class="geo-default">
             <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
              <span class="latitude">
               50°04′N
              </span>
              <span class="longitude">
               5°43′W
              </span>
             </span>
            </span>
            <span class="geo-multi-punct">
             ﻿ / ﻿
            </span>
            <span class="geo-nondefault">
             <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
              50.067°N 5.717°W
             </span>
             <span style="display:none">
              ﻿ /
              <span class="geo">
               50.067; -5.717
              </span>
             </span>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
          ).
         </span>
         <br/>
         <i>
          On the East.
         </i>
         <br/>
         <span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">
          The Southwestern limit of the
          <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
           North Sea
          </a>
          .
         </span>
        </p>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The IHO defines the southwestern limit of the North Sea as "a line joining the Walde Lighthouse (France, 1°55'E) and Leathercoat Point (England, 51°10'N)".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IHO-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Walde Lighthouse is 6 km east of
       <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">
        Calais
       </a>
       (
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=English_Channel&amp;params=50_59_06_N_1_55_00_E_">
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            50°59′06″N
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            1°55′00″E
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           50.98500°N 1.91667°E
          </span>
          <span style="display:none">
           ﻿ /
           <span class="geo">
            50.98500; 1.91667
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       ), and Leathercoat Point is at the north end of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Margaret%27s_Bay,_Kent" title="St Margaret's Bay, Kent">
        St Margaret's Bay, Kent
       </a>
       (
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=English_Channel&amp;params=51_10_00_N_1_24_00_E_">
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            51°10′00″N
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            1°24′00″E
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           51.16667°N 1.40000°E
          </span>
          <span style="display:none">
           ﻿ /
           <span class="geo">
            51.16667; 1.40000
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:France_manche_vue_dover.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="593" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/France_manche_vue_dover.JPG/220px-France_manche_vue_dover.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/France_manche_vue_dover.JPG/330px-France_manche_vue_dover.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/France_manche_vue_dover.JPG/440px-France_manche_vue_dover.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:France_manche_vue_dover.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Strait of Dover viewed from France, looking towards England. The
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_cliffs_of_Dover" title="White cliffs of Dover">
          white cliffs of Dover
         </a>
         on the English coast are visible from France on a clear day.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Dover" title="Strait of Dover">
        Strait of Dover
       </a>
       (French:
       <i>
        Pas de Calais
       </i>
       ), at the Channel's eastern end, is its narrowest point, while its widest point lies between
       <a href="/wiki/Lyme_Bay" title="Lyme Bay">
        Lyme Bay
       </a>
       and the Gulf of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Malo" title="Saint Malo">
        Saint Malo
       </a>
       , near its midpoint.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Columbia-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is relatively shallow, with an average depth of about 120 m (390 ft) at its widest part, reducing to a depth of about 45 m (148 ft) between
       <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">
        Dover
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">
        Calais
       </a>
       . Eastwards from there the adjoining North Sea reduces to about 26 m (85 ft) in the
       <a href="/wiki/Broad_Fourteens" title="Broad Fourteens">
        Broad Fourteens
       </a>
       where it lies over the watershed of the former land bridge between
       <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">
        East Anglia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">
        Low Countries
       </a>
       . It reaches a maximum depth of 180 m (590 ft) in the submerged valley of
       <a href="/wiki/Hurd%27s_Deep" title="Hurd's Deep">
        Hurd's Deep
       </a>
       , 48 km (30 mi) west-northwest of
       <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">
        Guernsey
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <span id="Bay_of_the_Seine2">
       </span>
       The eastern region along the French coast between
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">
        Cherbourg
       </a>
       and the mouth of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seine_river" title="Seine river">
        Seine river
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">
        Le Havre
       </a>
       is frequently referred to as the
       <i>
        Bay of the Seine
       </i>
       (French:
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         Baie de Seine
        </span>
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_French_river_mouths.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Three_French_river_mouths.JPG/220px-Three_French_river_mouths.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Three_French_river_mouths.JPG/330px-Three_French_river_mouths.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Three_French_river_mouths.JPG/440px-Three_French_river_mouths.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_French_river_mouths.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Three French river mouths. Top to bottom: the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Somme" title="River Somme">
          Somme
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Authie_(river)" title="Authie (river)">
          Authie
         </a>
         and the
         <a href="/wiki/Canche" title="Canche">
          Canche
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are several major islands in the Channel, the most notable being the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">
        Isle of Wight
       </a>
       off the English coast, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       , British
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">
        Crown dependencies
       </a>
       off the coast of France. The coastline, particularly on the French shore, is deeply indented; several small islands close to the coastline, including
       <a href="/wiki/Chausey" title="Chausey">
        Chausey
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" title="Mont Saint-Michel">
        Mont Saint-Michel
       </a>
       , are within French jurisdiction. The
       <a href="/wiki/Cotentin_Peninsula" title="Cotentin Peninsula">
        Cotentin Peninsula
       </a>
       in France juts out into the Channel, whilst on the English side there is a small parallel
       <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">
        strait
       </a>
       known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Solent" title="Solent">
        Solent
       </a>
       between the Isle of Wight and the mainland. The
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea" title="Celtic Sea">
        Celtic Sea
       </a>
       is to the west of the Channel.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Channel acts as a funnel that amplifies the tidal range from less than a metre as observed at sea
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2017)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       to more than 6 metres as observed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       , the west coast of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cotentin_Peninsula" title="Cotentin Peninsula">
        Cotentin Peninsula
       </a>
       and the north coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">
        Brittany
       </a>
       . The time difference of about six hours between high water at the eastern and western limits of the Channel is indicative of the tidal range being amplified further by
       <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">
        resonance
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the UK
       <a href="/wiki/Shipping_Forecast" title="Shipping Forecast">
        Shipping Forecast
       </a>
       the Channel is divided into the following areas, from the east:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">
         Dover
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">
         Wight
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Portland" title="Isle of Portland">
         Portland
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">
         Plymouth
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_etymology">
        History and etymology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History and etymology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="1032" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg/220px-Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg/330px-Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg/440px-Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Osborne-iow-3Ja10-10876.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">
          Osborne House
         </a>
         , the summer retreat of
         <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
          Queen Victoria
         </a>
         on the
         <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">
          Isle of Wight
         </a>
         . Starting from the late 18th century, settlements on and around the English Channel coastline in England grew rapidly into thriving
         <a href="/wiki/Seaside_resort" title="Seaside resort">
          seaside resorts
         </a>
         , bolstered by their association with royalty and the middle and upper classes.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Until the 18th century, the English Channel had no fixed name either in English or in French. It was never defined as a political border, and the names were more or less descriptive. It was not considered as the property of a nation. Strangely, before the development of the modern nations, British scholars very often referred to it as "Gaulish" (
       <i>
        Gallicum
       </i>
       in Latin) and French scholars as "British" or "English".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name "English Channel" has been widely used since the early 18th century, possibly originating from the designation
       <i>
        <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl">
         <i>
          Engelse Kanaal
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       in Dutch sea maps from the 16th century onwards. In modern Dutch, however, it is known as
       <i>
        <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl">
         <i>
          Het Kanaal
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       (with no reference to the word "English").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, it has also been known as the "British Channel"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or the "British Sea". It was called
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         <i>
          Oceanus Britannicus
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       by the 2nd-century geographer
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       . The same name is used on an Italian map of about 1450, which gives the alternative name of
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         <i>
          canalites Anglie
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       —possibly the first recorded use of the "Channel" designation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Anglo-Saxon texts often call it
       <i>
        Sūð-sǣ
       </i>
       ("South Sea") as opposed to
       <i>
        Norð-sǣ
       </i>
       ("North Sea" =
       <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">
        Bristol Channel
       </a>
       ). The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_word" title="Common word">
        common word
       </a>
       <i>
        channel
       </i>
       was first recorded in Middle English in the 13th century and was borrowed from Old French
       <i>
        chanel
       </i>
       , variant form of
       <i>
        chenel
       </i>
       "canal".
      </p>
      <p>
       The French name
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         <i>
          la Manche
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       has been in use since at least the 17th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name is usually said to refer to the Channel's sleeve (French:
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         la manche
        </span>
       </i>
       ) shape. Folk etymology has derived it from a
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       word meaning
       <i>
        channel
       </i>
       that is also the source of the name for
       <a href="/wiki/The_Minch" title="The Minch">
        the Minch
       </a>
       in Scotland,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but this name was never mentioned before the 17th century, and French and British sources of that time are perfectly clear about its etymology.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name in Breton (
       <i>
        Mor Breizh
       </i>
       ) means "Breton Sea", and its Cornish name (
       <i>
        Mor Bretannek
       </i>
       ) means "British Sea".
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_origins:_giant_waterfalls_and_catastrophic_floods">
        Geological origins: giant waterfalls and catastrophic floods
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geological origins: giant waterfalls and catastrophic floods">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Channel is of geologically recent origin, having been dry land for most of the
       <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
        Pleistocene
       </a>
       period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Before the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devensian_glaciation" title="Devensian glaciation">
        Devensian glaciation
       </a>
       (the most recent
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">
        ice age
       </a>
       , which ended around 10,000 years ago),
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">
        Britain
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
        Ireland
       </a>
       were part of
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">
        continental Europe
       </a>
       , linked by an unbroken
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weald-Artois_Anticline" title="Weald-Artois Anticline">
        Weald-Artois Anticline
       </a>
       , a ridge that acted as a natural dam holding back a large freshwater
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-glacial_lake" title="Pro-glacial lake">
        pro-glacial lake
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">
        Doggerland
       </a>
       region, now submerged under the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
        North Sea
       </a>
       . During this period the North Sea and almost all of the British Isles were covered by ice. The lake was fed by meltwater from the Baltic and from the Caledonian and Scandinavian
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">
        ice sheets
       </a>
       that joined to the north, blocking its exit. The sea level was about 120 m (390 ft) lower than it is today. Then, between 450,000 and 180,000 years ago, at least two catastrophic
       <a href="/wiki/Glacial_lake_outburst_flood" title="Glacial lake outburst flood">
        glacial lake outburst floods
       </a>
       breached the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weald%E2%80%93Artois_anticline" title="Weald–Artois anticline">
        Weald–Artois anticline
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The first flood would have lasted for several months, releasing as much as one million cubic metres of water per second.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_Nature_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gupta_Nature-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysToday_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhysToday-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The flood started with large but localized waterfalls over the ridge, which excavated depressions now known as the
       <i>
        Fosses
        <a href="/wiki/Louis_Dangeard" title="Louis Dangeard">
         Dangeard
        </a>
       </i>
       . The flow eroded the retaining ridge, causing the rock dam to fail and releasing lake water into the Atlantic. After multiple episodes of changing sea level, during which the
       <i>
        Fosses Dangeard
       </i>
       were largely infilled by various layers of sediment, another catastrophic flood carved a large bedrock-floored valley, the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lobourg_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lobourg Channel (page does not exist)">
        Lobourg Channel
       </a>
       , some 500 m wide and 25 m deep, from the southern North Sea basin through the centre of the Straits of Dover and into the English Channel. It left streamlined islands, longitudinal erosional grooves, and other features characteristic of catastrophic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaflood" title="Megaflood">
        megaflood
       </a>
       events, still present on the sea floor and now revealed by high-resolution sonar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Through the scoured channel passed a river, which drained the combined
       <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">
        Rhine
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thames" title="Thames">
        Thames
       </a>
       westwards to the Atlantic.
      </p>
      <p>
       The flooding destroyed the ridge that connected Britain to continental Europe, although a land connection across the southern
       <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
        North Sea
       </a>
       would have existed intermittently at later times when periods of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">
        glaciation
       </a>
       resulted in lowering of sea levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the end of the
       <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">
        last glacial period
       </a>
       , rising sea levels finally severed the last land connection.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Human_geography">
        Human geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Human geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="quotebox pullquote" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
       <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
        <div>
         This precious stone set in the silver sea,
         <br/>
         Which serves it in the office of a wall
         <br/>
         Or as a moat defensive to a house,
         <br/>
         Against the envy of less happier lands.
        </div>
       </div>
       <cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">
        <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">
         William Shakespeare
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">
          Richard II
         </a>
        </i>
        (Act II, Scene 1)
       </cite>
      </div>
      <p>
       The channel, which delayed human reoccupation of Great Britain for more than 100,000 years,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       has in historic times been both an easy entry for seafaring people and a key natural defence, halting invading armies while in conjunction with control of the North Sea allowing Britain to blockade the continent.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The most significant failed invasion threats came when the Dutch and Belgian ports were held by a major continental power, e.g. from the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">
        Spanish Armada
       </a>
       in 1588,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">
        Napoleon
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
        Napoleonic Wars
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
        Nazi Germany
       </a>
       during
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       . Successful invasions include the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">
        Roman conquest of Britain
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">
        Norman Conquest
       </a>
       in 1066, while the concentration of excellent harbours in the Western Channel on Britain's south coast made possible the largest invasion of all time, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normandy_Landings" title="Normandy Landings">
        Normandy Landings
       </a>
       in 1944. Channel
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_naval_battles" title="List of naval battles">
        naval battles
       </a>
       include the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Downs" title="Battle of the Downs">
        Battle of the Downs
       </a>
       (1639),
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Goodwin_Sands" title="Battle of Goodwin Sands">
        Battle of Goodwin Sands
       </a>
       (1652), the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Portland" title="Battle of Portland">
        Battle of Portland
       </a>
       (1653), the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Hougue" title="Battle of La Hougue">
        Battle of La Hougue
       </a>
       (1692) and the engagement between
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Kearsarge_(1861)" title="USS Kearsarge (1861)">
        USS
        <i>
         Kearsarge
        </i>
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/CSS_Alabama" title="CSS Alabama">
        CSS
        <i>
         Alabama
        </i>
       </a>
       (1864).
      </p>
      <p>
       In more peaceful times the Channel served as a link joining shared cultures and political structures, particularly the huge
       <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">
        Angevin Empire
       </a>
       from 1135 to 1217. For nearly a thousand years, the Channel also provided a link between the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Celts" title="Modern Celts">
        Modern Celtic
       </a>
       regions and languages of
       <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">
        Cornwall
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">
        Brittany
       </a>
       . Brittany was founded by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Britons" title="Ancient Britons">
        Britons
       </a>
       who fled
       <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">
        Cornwall
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">
        Devon
       </a>
       after Anglo-Saxon encroachment. In Brittany, there is a region known as "
       <a href="/wiki/Cornouaille" title="Cornouaille">
        Cornouaille
       </a>
       " (Cornwall) in French and "Kernev" in
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In ancient times there was also a "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domnonia" title="Domnonia">
        Domnonia
       </a>
       " (Devon) in Brittany as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       In February 1684, ice formed on the sea in a belt 3 miles (4.8 km) wide off the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
        Kent
       </a>
       and 2 miles (3.2 km) wide on the French side.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Route_to_Britain">
        Route to Britain
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Route to Britain">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_sea_languages_900.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="348" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/North_sea_languages_900.png/220px-North_sea_languages_900.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/North_sea_languages_900.png/330px-North_sea_languages_900.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/North_sea_languages_900.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_sea_languages_900.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The approximate extent of Old Norse and related languages in the early 10th century around the North Sea. The red area is the distribution of the dialect
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_West_Norse#Old_West_Norse" title="Old West Norse">
          Old West Norse
         </a>
         , the orange area
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_East_Norse" title="Old East Norse">
          Old East Norse
         </a>
         , and the green area the other Germanic languages with which Old Norse still retained some mutual intelligibility.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Remnants of a
       <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">
        mesolithic
       </a>
       boatyard have been found on the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">
        Isle of Wight
       </a>
       . Wheat was traded across the Channel about 8,000 years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "... Sophisticated social networks linked the
       <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">
        Neolithic
       </a>
       front in southern Europe to the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">
        Mesolithic
       </a>
       peoples of northern Europe." The
       <a href="/wiki/Ferriby_Boats" title="Ferriby Boats">
        Ferriby Boats
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hanson_Log_Boat" title="Hanson Log Boat">
        Hanson Log Boats
       </a>
       and the later
       <a href="/wiki/Dover_Bronze_Age_Boat" title="Dover Bronze Age Boat">
        Dover Bronze Age Boat
       </a>
       could carry a substantial cross-Channel cargo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">
        Diodorus Siculus
       </a>
       and Pliny
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       both suggest trade between the rebel Celtic tribes of
       <a href="/wiki/Armorica" title="Armorica">
        Armorica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
        Iron Age
       </a>
       Britain flourished. In 55 BC
       <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">
        Julius Caesar
       </a>
       invaded, claiming that the Britons had aided the
       <a href="/wiki/Veneti_(Gaul)" title="Veneti (Gaul)">
        Veneti
       </a>
       against him the previous year. He was more successful in 54 BC, but Britain was not fully established as part of the Roman Empire until completion of the invasion by
       <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Plautius" title="Aulus Plautius">
        Aulus Plautius
       </a>
       in 43 AD. A brisk and regular trade began between ports in Roman
       <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">
        Gaul
       </a>
       and those in Britain. This traffic continued until the
       <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">
        end of Roman rule in Britain
       </a>
       in 410 AD, after which the
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anglo-Saxon_England" title="History of Anglo-Saxon England">
        early Anglo-Saxons
       </a>
       left less clear historical records.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the power vacuum left by the retreating Romans, the Germanic
       <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">
        Angles
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">
        Saxons
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">
        Jutes
       </a>
       began the next great migration across the North Sea. Having already been used as mercenaries in Britain by the Romans, many people from these tribes crossed during the
       <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">
        Migration Period
       </a>
       , conquering and perhaps displacing the native
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">
        Celtic
       </a>
       populations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norsemen_and_Normans">
        Norsemen and Normans
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Norsemen and Normans">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1290" data-file-width="1041" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg/220px-Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg/330px-Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg/440px-Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hermitage_St_Helier_Jersey.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Hermitage of
         <a href="/wiki/Helier" title="Helier">
          St Helier
         </a>
         lies in the bay off
         <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helier" title="Saint Helier">
          Saint Helier
         </a>
         and is accessible on foot at low tide.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The attack on
       <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">
        Lindisfarne
       </a>
       in 793 is generally considered the beginning of the
       <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">
        Viking Age
       </a>
       . For the next 250 years the Scandinavian raiders of Norway, Sweden, and Denmark dominated the North Sea, raiding monasteries, homes, and towns along the coast and along the rivers that ran inland. According to the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">
         Anglo-Saxon Chronicle
        </a>
       </i>
       they began to settle in Britain in 851. They continued to settle in the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">
        British Isles
       </a>
       and the continent until around 1050.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">
        fiefdom of Normandy
       </a>
       was created for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">
        Viking
       </a>
       leader
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rollo_of_Normandy" title="Rollo of Normandy">
        Rollo
       </a>
       (also known as Robert of Normandy). Rollo had besieged Paris but in 911 entered
       <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">
        vassalage
       </a>
       to the king of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Francia" title="Western Francia">
        West Franks
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Simple" title="Charles the Simple">
        Charles the Simple
       </a>
       through the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_St.-Claire-sur-Epte" title="Treaty of St.-Claire-sur-Epte">
        Treaty of St.-Claire-sur-Epte
       </a>
       . In exchange for his
       <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">
        homage
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fealty" title="Fealty">
        fealty
       </a>
       , Rollo legally gained the territory he and his Viking allies had previously conquered. The name "Normandy" reflects Rollo's Viking (i.e. "Northman") origins.
      </p>
      <p>
       The descendants of Rollo and his followers adopted the local
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance_languages" title="Gallo-Romance languages">
        Gallo-Romance language
       </a>
       and intermarried with the area's inhabitants and became the
       <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">
        Normans
       </a>
       – a
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
        Norman French
       </a>
       -speaking mixture of
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">
        Scandinavians
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norse" title="Hiberno-Norse">
        Hiberno-Norse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">
        Orcadians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">
        Anglo-Danish
       </a>
       , and indigenous
       <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">
        Franks
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">
        Gauls
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Rollo's descendant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" title="William I of England">
        William, Duke of Normandy
       </a>
       became king of England in 1066 in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">
        Norman Conquest
       </a>
       beginning with the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">
        Battle of Hastings
       </a>
       , while retaining the fiefdom of Normandy for himself and his descendants. In 1204, during the reign of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_England" title="John of England">
        King John
       </a>
       , mainland Normandy was taken from England by France under
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">
        Philip II
       </a>
       , while insular Normandy (the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       ) remained under English control. In 1259,
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">
        Henry III of England
       </a>
       recognised the legality of French possession of mainland Normandy under the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1259)" title="Treaty of Paris (1259)">
        Treaty of Paris
       </a>
       . His successors, however, often fought to regain control of mainland Normandy.
      </p>
      <p>
       With the rise of
       <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">
        William the Conqueror
       </a>
       the North Sea and Channel began to lose some of their importance. The new order oriented most of England and Scandinavia's trade south, toward the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       and the Orient.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the British surrendered claims to mainland Normandy and other French possessions in 1801, the monarch of the United Kingdom retains the title Duke of Normandy in respect to the Channel Islands. The Channel Islands (except for
       <a href="/wiki/Chausey" title="Chausey">
        Chausey
       </a>
       ) are
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">
        Crown dependencies
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">
        British Crown
       </a>
       . Thus the
       <a href="/wiki/Loyal_toast" title="Loyal toast">
        Loyal toast
       </a>
       in the Channel Islands is
       <i>
        La Reine, notre Duc
       </i>
       ("The Queen, our Duke"). The British monarch is understood to
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       be the Duke of Normandy in regards of the French region of Normandy described herein, by virtue of the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1259)" title="Treaty of Paris (1259)">
        Treaty of Paris of 1259
       </a>
       , the surrender of French possessions in 1801, and the belief that the rights of succession to that title are subject to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salic_Law" title="Salic Law">
        Salic Law
       </a>
       which excludes inheritance through female heirs.
      </p>
      <p>
       French Normandy was occupied by English forces during the
       <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">
        Hundred Years' War
       </a>
       in 1346–1360 and again in 1415–1450.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="England_and_Britain:_Naval_superpower">
        England and Britain: Naval superpower
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: England and Britain: Naval superpower">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       From the reign of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">
        Elizabeth I
       </a>
       , English foreign policy concentrated on preventing invasion across the Channel by ensuring no major European power controlled the potential Dutch and Flemish invasion ports. Her climb to the pre-eminent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_power" title="Sea power">
        sea power
       </a>
       of the world began in 1588 as the attempted invasion of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">
        Spanish Armada
       </a>
       was defeated by the combination of outstanding naval tactics by the English and the Dutch under command of
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Howard,_1st_Earl_of_Nottingham" title="Charles Howard, 1st Earl of Nottingham">
        Charles Howard, 1st Earl of Nottingham
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">
        Sir Francis Drake
       </a>
       second in command, and the following stormy weather. Over the centuries the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
        Royal Navy
       </a>
       slowly grew to be the most powerful in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The building of the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
        British Empire
       </a>
       was possible only because the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
        Royal Navy
       </a>
       eventually managed to exercise unquestioned control over the seas around Europe, especially the Channel and the North Sea. During the
       <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">
        Seven Years' War
       </a>
       , France attempted to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planned_French_Invasion_of_Britain_(1759)" title="Planned French Invasion of Britain (1759)">
        launch an invasion of Britain
       </a>
       . To achieve this France needed to gain control of the Channel for several weeks, but was thwarted following the British naval victory at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Quiberon_Bay" title="Battle of Quiberon Bay">
        Battle of Quiberon Bay
       </a>
       in 1759.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another significant challenge to British domination of the seas came during the
       <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
        Napoleonic Wars
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">
        Battle of Trafalgar
       </a>
       took place off the coast of Spain against a combined French and Spanish fleet and was won by Admiral
       <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson" title="Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson">
        Horatio Nelson
       </a>
       , ending
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">
        Napoleon
       </a>
       's plans for a cross-Channel invasion and securing British dominance of the seas for over a century.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">
        First World War
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: First World War">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The exceptional strategic importance of the Channel as a tool for blockade was recognised by the First Sea Lord
       <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">
        Admiral Fisher
       </a>
       in the years before
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       . "Five keys lock up the world! Singapore, the Cape,
       <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">
        Alexandria
       </a>
       , Gibraltar, Dover."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, on 25 July 1909
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_Bl%C3%A9riot" title="Louis Blériot">
        Louis Blériot
       </a>
       made the first Channel crossing from
       <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">
        Calais
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">
        Dover
       </a>
       in an aeroplane. Blériot's crossing signalled the end of the Channel as a barrier-moat for England against foreign enemies.
      </p>
      <p>
       Because the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaiserliche_Marine" title="Kaiserliche Marine">
         Kaiserliche Marine
        </a>
       </i>
       surface fleet could not match the British Grand Fleet, the
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germans
       </a>
       developed
       <a href="/wiki/Submarine_warfare" title="Submarine warfare">
        submarine warfare
       </a>
       , which was to become a far greater threat to Britain. The
       <a href="/wiki/Dover_Patrol" title="Dover Patrol">
        Dover Patrol
       </a>
       was set up just before the war started to escort cross-Channel troopships and to prevent submarines from sailing in the Channel, obliging them to travel to the Atlantic via the much longer route around
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       On land, the German army attempted to capture Channel ports in the
       <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_Sea" title="Race to the Sea">
        Race to the Sea
       </a>
       but although the trenches are often said to have stretched "from the frontier of Switzerland to the English Channel", they reached the coast at the North Sea. Much of the British war effort in
       <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">
        Flanders
       </a>
       was a bloody but successful strategy to prevent the Germans reaching the Channel coast.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the outset of the war, an attempt was made to block the path of
       <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">
        U-boats
       </a>
       through the Dover Strait with
       <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">
        naval minefields
       </a>
       . By February 1915, this had been augmented by a 25 kilometres (16 mi) stretch of light steel netting called the
       <a href="/wiki/Dover_Barrage" title="Dover Barrage">
        Dover Barrage
       </a>
       , which it was hoped would ensnare submerged submarines. After initial success, the Germans learned how to pass through the barrage, aided by the unreliability of British mines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 31 January 1917, the Germans restarted
       <a href="/wiki/Unrestricted_submarine_warfare" title="Unrestricted submarine warfare">
        unrestricted submarine warfare
       </a>
       leading to dire Admiralty predictions that submarines would defeat Britain by November,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the most dangerous situation Britain faced in either world war.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Battle of Passchendaele">
        Battle of Passchendaele
       </a>
       in 1917 was fought to reduce the threat by capturing the submarine bases on the Belgian coast, though it was the introduction of
       <a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">
        convoys
       </a>
       and not capture of the bases that averted defeat. In April 1918 the
       <a href="/wiki/Dover_Patrol" title="Dover Patrol">
        Dover Patrol
       </a>
       carried out the famous
       <a href="/wiki/Zeebrugge_Raid" title="Zeebrugge Raid">
        Zeebrugge Raid
       </a>
       against the
       <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">
        U-boat
       </a>
       bases. During 1917, the Dover Barrage was re-sited with improved mines and more effective nets, aided by regular patrols by small warships equipped with powerful searchlights. A German attack on these vessels resulted in the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dover_Strait_(1917)" title="Battle of Dover Strait (1917)">
        Battle of Dover Strait in 1917
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A much more ambitious attempt to improve the barrage, by installing eight massive concrete towers across the strait was called the
       <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_M-N_Scheme" title="Admiralty M-N Scheme">
        Admiralty M-N Scheme
       </a>
       but only two towers were nearing completion at the end of the war and the project was abandoned.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The naval blockade in the Channel and North Sea was one of the decisive factors in the German defeat in 1918.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">
        Second World War
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Second World War">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UK_Radar1940.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1670" data-file-width="1400" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/UK_Radar1940.JPG/220px-UK_Radar1940.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/UK_Radar1940.JPG/330px-UK_Radar1940.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/UK_Radar1940.JPG/440px-UK_Radar1940.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UK_Radar1940.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         British radar facilities during the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">
          Battle of Britain
         </a>
         1940
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
        Second World War
       </a>
       , naval activity in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Theatre_of_World_War_II" title="European Theatre of World War II">
        European theatre
       </a>
       was primarily
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">
        limited to the Atlantic
       </a>
       . During the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">
        Battle of France
       </a>
       in May 1940, the German forces succeeded in capturing both
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Boulogne_(1940)" title="Battle of Boulogne (1940)">
        Boulogne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Calais_(1940)" title="Siege of Calais (1940)">
        Calais
       </a>
       , thereby threatening the line of retreat for the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_II)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War II)">
        British Expeditionary Force
       </a>
       . By a combination of hard fighting and German indecision, the port of
       <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk" title="Dunkirk">
        Dunkirk
       </a>
       was kept open allowing 338,000 Allied troops to be evacuated in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Dynamo" title="Operation Dynamo">
        Operation Dynamo
       </a>
       . More than 11,000 were evacuated from
       <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">
        Le Havre
       </a>
       during
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cycle" title="Operation Cycle">
        Operation Cycle
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a further 192,000 were evacuated from ports further down the coast in
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Ariel" title="Operation Ariel">
        Operation Ariel
       </a>
       in June 1940.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The early stages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">
        Battle of Britain
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       featured air attacks on Channel shipping and ports, and until the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normandy_Landings" title="Normandy Landings">
        Normandy Landings
       </a>
       (with the exception of the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Dash" title="Channel Dash">
        Channel Dash
       </a>
       ) the narrow waters were too dangerous for major warships. Despite these early successes against shipping, the Germans did not win the
       <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">
        air supremacy
       </a>
       necessary for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Sealion" title="Operation Sealion">
        Operation Sealion
       </a>
       , the projected cross-Channel invasion.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Channel subsequently became the stage for an intensive coastal war, featuring submarines,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" title="Minesweeper (ship)">
        minesweepers
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_Attack_Craft" title="Fast Attack Craft">
        Fast Attack Craft
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="800" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg/220px-NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg/330px-NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg/440px-NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NormandyCourcelles2JM.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         150 mm Second World War German gun emplacement in Normandy
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1089" data-file-width="1513" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg/220px-German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg/330px-German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg/440px-German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_World_War_II_tower_Jersey.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         As part of the
         <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Wall" title="Atlantic Wall">
          Atlantic Wall
         </a>
         , between 1940 and 1945 the occupying
         <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">
          German forces
         </a>
         and the
         <a href="/wiki/Organisation_Todt" title="Organisation Todt">
          Organisation Todt
         </a>
         constructed fortifications round the coasts of the Channel Islands, such as this observation tower at Les Landes, Jersey.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dieppe,_Seine-Maritime" title="Dieppe, Seine-Maritime">
        Dieppe
       </a>
       was the site of an ill-fated
       <a href="/wiki/Dieppe_Raid" title="Dieppe Raid">
        Dieppe Raid
       </a>
       by Canadian and British armed forces. More successful was the later
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">
        Operation Overlord
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-Day" title="D-Day">
        D-Day
       </a>
       ), a massive invasion of
       <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
        German
       </a>
       -occupied France by
       <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">
        Allied
       </a>
       troops.
       <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">
        Caen
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">
        Cherbourg
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carentan" title="Carentan">
        Carentan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Falaise,_Calvados" title="Falaise, Calvados">
        Falaise
       </a>
       and other Norman towns endured many casualties in the fight for the province, which continued until the closing of the so-called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falaise_pocket" title="Falaise pocket">
        Falaise gap
       </a>
       between
       <a href="/wiki/Chambois,_Orne" title="Chambois, Orne">
        Chambois
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Montormel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Montormel (page does not exist)">
        Montormel
       </a>
       , then liberation of
       <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">
        Le Havre
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Channel Islands were the only part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">
        British Commonwealth
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_the_Channel_Islands" title="German occupation of the Channel Islands">
        occupied by Germany
       </a>
       (excepting the part of
       <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
        Egypt
       </a>
       occupied by the
       <a href="/wiki/Afrika_Korps" title="Afrika Korps">
        Afrika Korps
       </a>
       at the time of the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_El_Alamein" title="Second Battle of El Alamein">
        Second Battle of El Alamein
       </a>
       , which was a protectorate and not part of the Commonwealth). The German occupation of 1940–1945 was harsh, with some island residents being taken for
       <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour_under_German_rule_during_World_War_II" title="Forced labour under German rule during World War II">
        slave labour
       </a>
       on the Continent; native Jews sent to
       <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">
        concentration camps
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Partisan_(military)" title="Partisan (military)">
        partisan
       </a>
       resistance and retribution; accusations of
       <a href="/wiki/Collaborationism" title="Collaborationism">
        collaboration
       </a>
       ; and slave labour (primarily Russians and eastern Europeans) being brought to the islands to build fortifications.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
        Royal Navy
       </a>
       blockaded the islands from time to time, particularly following the
       <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Normandy" title="Invasion of Normandy">
        liberation of mainland Normandy
       </a>
       in 1944. Intense negotiations resulted in some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Cross" title="Red Cross">
        Red Cross
       </a>
       humanitarian aid, but there was considerable hunger and privation during the occupation, particularly in the final months, when the population was close to starvation. The German troops on the islands surrendered on 9 May 1945, a day after the final surrender in mainland Europe.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Population">
        Population
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Population">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The English Channel coast is far more densely populated on the English shore. The most significant towns and cities along both the English and French sides of the Channel (each with more than 20,000 inhabitants, ranked in descending order; populations are the urban area populations from the 1999 French census, 2001 UK census, and 2001
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </a>
       census) are as follows:
      </p>
